<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Lake</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Large body of relatively still water</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Lake_(disambiguation)" title="Lake (disambiguation)">Lake (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Lakes_(disambiguation)" title="Lakes (disambiguation)">Lakes (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gentau_Pic_du_Midi_Ossau.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2717" data-file-width="4154" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Gentau_Pic_du_Midi_Ossau.jpg/330px-Gentau_Pic_du_Midi_Ossau.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Gentau_Pic_du_Midi_Ossau.jpg/495px-Gentau_Pic_du_Midi_Ossau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Gentau_Pic_du_Midi_Ossau.jpg/660px-Gentau_Pic_du_Midi_Ossau.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gentau_Pic_du_Midi_Ossau.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lac_Gentau" title="Lac Gentau">Lac Gentau</a> lake at the <a href="/wiki/Ossau_Valley" title="Ossau Valley">Ossau Valley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees, France</a></div></div></div>
<p>A <b>lake</b> is an area filled with water, localized in a <a href="/wiki/Depression_(geology)" title="Depression (geology)">basin</a>, surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Land" title="Land">land</a>, and distinct from any <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">river</a> or other outlet that serves to feed or drain the lake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Lakes lie on land and are not part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Ocean" title="World Ocean">ocean</a>, although, like the much larger oceans, they do form part of the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a>. Lakes are distinct from <a href="/wiki/Lagoon" title="Lagoon">lagoons</a>, which are generally coastal parts of the ocean. Lakes are typically larger and deeper than <a href="/wiki/Pond" title="Pond">ponds</a>, which also lie on land, though there are no official or scientific definitions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Lakes can be contrasted with <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a> or <a href="/wiki/Stream" title="Stream">streams</a>, which usually flow in a channel on land. Most lakes are fed and drained by rivers and streams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenh_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-fenh-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural lakes are generally found in mountainous areas, <a href="/wiki/Rift_zone" title="Rift zone">rift zones</a>, and areas with ongoing <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciation</a>. Other lakes are found in <a href="/wiki/Endorheic_basin" title="Endorheic basin">endorheic basins</a> or along the courses of mature rivers, where a river channel has widened into a basin. Some parts of the world have many lakes formed by the chaotic drainage patterns left over from the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Period" title="Last Glacial Period">last ice age</a>. All lakes are temporary over <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">long periods of time</a>, as they will slowly fill in with sediments or spill out of the basin containing them.
</p><p>Many lakes are <a href="/wiki/Controlled_lake" title="Controlled lake">artificial</a> and are constructed for industrial or agricultural use, for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydro-electric_power" title="Hydro-electric power">hydro-electric power</a> generation or domestic water supply, for aesthetic or recreational purposes, or for other activities.
</p>

<h2><span id="Etymology.2C_meaning.2C_and_usage_of_.22lake.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Etymology,_meaning,_and_usage_of_"lake"'>Etymology, meaning, and usage of "lake"</span></h2>
<p>The word <i>lake</i> comes from <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> <span title="Middle English (1100-1500)-language text"><i lang="enm">lake</i></span> ('lake, pond, waterway'), from <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <span title="Old English (ca. 450-1100)-language text"><i lang="ang">lacu</i></span> ('pond, pool, stream'), from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a> <span title="Proto-Germanic-language text">*<i lang="gem">lakō</i></span> ('pond, ditch, slow moving stream'), from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> root <span title="Proto-Indo-European-language text">*<i lang="ine">leǵ-</i></span> ('to leak, drain'). Cognates include <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> <span title="Dutch-language text"><i lang="nl">laak</i></span> ('lake, pond, ditch'), <a href="/wiki/Middle_Low_German" title="Middle Low German">Middle Low German</a> <span title="Middle Low German-language text"><i lang="gml">lāke</i></span> ('water pooled in a riverbed, puddle') as in: <a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wolfslake" title="de:Wolfslake">de:Wolfslake</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Butterlake" title="de:Butterlake">de:Butterlake</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Lache</i></span> ('pool, puddle'), and <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a> <span title="Icelandic-language text"><i lang="is">lækur</i></span> ('slow flowing stream'). Also related are the English words <i>leak</i> and <i>leach</i>.
</p><p>There is considerable uncertainty about defining the difference between lakes and <a href="/wiki/Pond" title="Pond">ponds</a>, and neither term has an internationally accepted definition across scientific disciplines or political boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeinonenZiglio2008_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-HeinonenZiglio2008-4">[4]</a></sup> For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limnologist" title="Limnologist">limnologists</a> have defined lakes as water bodies that are simply a larger version of a pond, which can have wave action on the shoreline or where wind-induced turbulence plays a major role in mixing the water column. None of these definitions completely excludes ponds and all are difficult to measure. For this reason, simple size-based definitions are increasingly used to separate ponds and lakes. Definitions for <i>lake</i> range in minimum sizes for a body of water from 2 hectares (5 acres)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BiologicalConservation1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-BiologicalConservation1-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 331">: 331 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BiologicalReviews1_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-BiologicalReviews1-6">[6]</a></sup> to 8 hectares (20 acres).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Pioneering animal ecologist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sutherland_Elton" title="Charles Sutherland Elton">Charles Elton</a> regarded lakes as waterbodies of 40 hectares (99 acres) or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JournalOfEcology1_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-JournalOfEcology1-8">[8]</a></sup> The term <i>lake</i> is also used to describe a feature such as <a href="/wiki/Lake_Eyre" title="Lake Eyre">Lake Eyre</a>, which is a dry basin most of the time but may become filled under seasonal conditions of heavy rainfall. In common usage, many lakes bear names ending with the word <i>pond</i>, and a lesser number of names ending with <i>lake</i> are, in quasi-technical fact, ponds. One textbook illustrates this point with the following: "In Newfoundland, for example, almost every lake is called a pond, whereas in Wisconsin, almost every pond is called a lake."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cech2009_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cech2009-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>One <a href="/wiki/Hydrology" title="Hydrology">hydrology</a> book proposes to define the term "lake" as a body of water with the following five characteristics:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HeinonenZiglio2008_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-HeinonenZiglio2008-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>It partially or totally fills one or several basins connected by <a href="/wiki/Strait" title="Strait">straits</a>;</li>
<li>It has essentially the same water level in all parts (except for relatively short-lived variations caused by wind, varying ice cover, large inflows, etc.);</li>
<li>It does not have regular intrusion of <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a>;</li>
<li>A considerable portion of the <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a> suspended in the water is captured by the basins (for this to happen they need to have a sufficiently small inflow-to-volume ratio);</li>
<li>The area measured at the mean water level exceeds an arbitrarily chosen threshold (for instance, one <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">hectare</a>).</li></ol>
<p>With the exception of criterion 3, the others have been accepted or elaborated upon by other hydrology publications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shahin2002_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shahin2002-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copernicus2004_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Copernicus2004-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eyre_lake_map_of_the_shape_and_depth_(bathymetry)_2020.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="545" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Eyre_lake_map_of_the_shape_and_depth_%28bathymetry%29_2020.jpg/220px-Eyre_lake_map_of_the_shape_and_depth_%28bathymetry%29_2020.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Eyre_lake_map_of_the_shape_and_depth_%28bathymetry%29_2020.jpg/330px-Eyre_lake_map_of_the_shape_and_depth_%28bathymetry%29_2020.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Eyre_lake_map_of_the_shape_and_depth_%28bathymetry%29_2020.jpg/440px-Eyre_lake_map_of_the_shape_and_depth_%28bathymetry%29_2020.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eyre_lake_map_of_the_shape_and_depth_(bathymetry)_2020.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lake_Eyre" title="Lake Eyre">Lake Eyre</a>'s shape and depth as a gradient map</div></div></div>
<p>The majority of lakes on Earth are <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">freshwater</a>, and most lie in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> at higher <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitudes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Drainage_system_(geomorphology)#Deranged_drainage_pattern" title="Drainage system (geomorphology)">deranged drainage system</a>, has an estimated 31,752 lakes larger than 3 square kilometres (1.2 sq mi) in surface area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The total number of lakes in Canada is unknown but is estimated to be at least 2 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> has 187,888 lakes of 500 square metres (5,400 sq ft) in area, or larger, of which 56,000 are large (10,000 square metres (110,000 sq ft) or larger).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most lakes have at least one natural outflow in the form of a <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">river</a> or <a href="/wiki/Stream" title="Stream">stream</a>, which maintain a lake's average level by allowing the drainage of excess water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenh_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-fenh-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Some lakes do not have a natural outflow and lose water solely by evaporation or underground seepage, or both. These are termed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endorheic" title="Endorheic">endorheic</a> lakes.
</p><p>Many lakes are artificial and are constructed for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydro-electric" title="Hydro-electric">hydro-electric</a> power generation, aesthetic purposes, <a href="/wiki/Recreation" title="Recreation">recreational</a> purposes, industrial use, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agricultural" title="Agricultural">agricultural</a> use, or domestic <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">water supply</a>.
</p><p>The number of lakes on Earth is undetermined because most lakes and ponds are very small and do not appear on maps or <a href="/wiki/Satellite_imagery" title="Satellite imagery">satellite imagery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DowningPrairie2006_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-DowningPrairie2006-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Despite this uncertainty, a large number of studies agree that small ponds are much more abundant than large lakes. For example, one widely cited study estimated that Earth has 304 million lakes and ponds, and that 91% of these are 1 hectare (2.5 acres) or less in area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DowningPrairie2006_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-DowningPrairie2006-17">[17]</a></sup> Despite the overwhelming abundance of ponds, almost all of Earth's lake water is found in fewer than 100 large lakes; this is because lake volume <a href="/wiki/Power_law" title="Power law">scales</a> superlinearly with lake area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Extraterrestrial lakes exists on the moon <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, which orbits the planet <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The shape of lakes on Titan is very similar to those on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Lakes were formerly present on the surface of Mars, but are now <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dry_lake_bed" title="Dry lake bed">dry lake beds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h2>
<p>In 1957, <a href="/wiki/G._Evelyn_Hutchinson" title="G. Evelyn Hutchinson">Hutchinson</a> published a monograph titled <i>A Treatise on Limnology</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson1957a_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson1957a-27">[27]</a></sup> which is regarded as a landmark discussion and classification of all major lake types, their origin, morphometric characteristics, and distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2003a_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2003a-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Håkanson2012a_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Håkanson2012a-30">[30]</a></sup> Hutchinson presented in his publication a comprehensive analysis of the origin of lakes and proposed what is a widely accepted classification of lakes according to their origin. This classification recognizes 11 major lake types that are divided into 76 subtypes. The 11 major lake types are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2003a_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2003a-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Håkanson2012a_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Håkanson2012a-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 12em;">
<ul><li>tectonic lakes</li>
<li>volcanic lakes</li>
<li>glacial lakes</li>
<li>fluvial lakes</li>
<li>solution lakes</li>
<li>landslide lakes</li>
<li>aeolian lakes</li>
<li>shoreline lakes</li>
<li>organic lakes</li>
<li>anthropogenic lakes</li>
<li>meteorite (extraterrestrial impact) lakes</li></ul>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tectonic_lakes">Tectonic lakes</span></h3>
<p>Tectonic lakes are lakes formed by the deformation and resulting lateral and vertical movements of the Earth's crust. These movements include faulting, tilting, folding, and warping. Some of the largest lakes on Earth are <a href="/wiki/Rift_lake" title="Rift lake">rift lakes</a> occupying rift valleys, e.g. Central African Rift lakes and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a>. Other well-known tectonic lakes, <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_of_Aral" title="Sea of Aral">Sea of Aral</a>, and other lakes from the Pontocaspian occupy basins that have been separated from the sea by the tectonic uplift of the sea floor above the ocean level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson1957a_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson1957a-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2003a_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2003a-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Håkanson2012a_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Håkanson2012a-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Often, the tectonic action of crustal extension has created an alternating series of parallel <a href="/wiki/Graben" title="Graben">grabens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Horst_(geology)" title="Horst (geology)">horsts</a> that form elongate basins alternating with mountain ranges. Not only does this promote the creation of lakes by the disruption of preexisting drainage networks, it also creates within arid regions <a href="/wiki/Endorheic_basin" title="Endorheic basin">endorheic basins</a> that contain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_lake_(geography)" title="Salt lake (geography)">salt lakes</a> (also called <a href="/wiki/Saline_water" title="Saline water">saline</a> lakes). They form where there is no natural outlet, a high evaporation rate and the drainage surface of the <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water table</a> has a higher-than-normal <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a> content. Examples of these salt lakes include <a href="/wiki/Great_Salt_Lake" title="Great Salt Lake">Great Salt Lake</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">Dead Sea</a>. Another type of tectonic lake caused by faulting is <a href="/wiki/Sag_pond" title="Sag pond">sag ponds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson1957a_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson1957a-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2003a_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2003a-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Håkanson2012a_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-Håkanson2012a-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Volcanic_lakes">Volcanic lakes</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gunung_rinjani.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Gunung_rinjani.jpg/220px-Gunung_rinjani.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Gunung_rinjani.jpg/330px-Gunung_rinjani.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Gunung_rinjani.jpg/440px-Gunung_rinjani.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gunung_rinjani.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The crater lake of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Rinjani" title="Mount Rinjani">Mount Rinjani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Volcanogenic_lake" title="Volcanogenic lake">Volcanogenic lake</a></div>
<p>Volcanic lakes are lakes that occupy either local depressions, e.g. craters and <a href="/wiki/Maar" title="Maar">maars</a>, or larger basins, e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calderas" title="Calderas">calderas</a>, created by <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">volcanism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_crater_lake" title="Volcanic crater lake">Crater lakes</a> are formed in <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_crater" title="Volcanic crater">volcanic craters</a> and calderas, which fill up with precipitation more rapidly than they empty via either evaporation, groundwater discharge, or a combination of both. Sometimes the latter are called caldera lakes, although often no distinction is made. An example is <a href="/wiki/Crater_Lake" title="Crater Lake">Crater Lake</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, in the caldera of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Mazama" title="Mount Mazama">Mount Mazama</a>. The caldera was created in a massive volcanic eruption that led to the <a href="/wiki/Subsidence" title="Subsidence">subsidence</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Mazama" title="Mount Mazama">Mount Mazama</a> around 4860 BCE. Other volcanic lakes are created when either rivers or streams are dammed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lava_flow" title="Lava flow">lava flows</a> or volcanic <a href="/wiki/Lahar" title="Lahar">lahars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson1957a_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson1957a-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-4"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2003a_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2003a-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Håkanson2012a_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-Håkanson2012a-30">[30]</a></sup> The basin which is now <a href="/wiki/Malheur_Lake" title="Malheur Lake">Malheur Lake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a> was created when a lava flow dammed the <a href="/wiki/Malheur_River" title="Malheur River">Malheur River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1985_lake_atlas_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-1985_lake_atlas-31">[31]</a></sup> Among all lake types, volcanic crater lakes most closely approximate a circular shape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenh_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-fenh-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glacial_lakes">Glacial lakes</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lake_Kaniere.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1591" data-file-width="3912" decoding="async" height="89" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Lake_Kaniere.jpg/220px-Lake_Kaniere.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Lake_Kaniere.jpg/330px-Lake_Kaniere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Lake_Kaniere.jpg/440px-Lake_Kaniere.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lake_Kaniere.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lake_Kaniere" title="Lake Kaniere">Lake Kaniere</a> is a glacial lake in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Coast,_New_Zealand" title="West Coast, New Zealand">West Coast</a> region of <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Glacial_lake" title="Glacial lake">Glacial lake</a></div>
<p>Glacial lakes are lakes created by the direct action of glaciers and continental ice sheets. A wide variety of glacial processes create enclosed basins. As a result, there are a wide variety of different types of glacial lakes and it is often difficult to define clear-cut distinctions between different types of glacial lakes and lakes influenced by other activities. The general types of glacial lakes that have been recognized are lakes in direct contact with ice, glacially carved rock basins and depressions, morainic and outwash lakes, and glacial drift basins. Glacial lakes are the most numerous lakes in the world. Most lakes in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">northern Europe</a> and North America have been either influenced or created by the latest, but not last, glaciation, to have covered the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson1957a_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson1957a-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-5"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2003a_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2003a-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Håkanson2012a_30-5"><a href="#cite_note-Håkanson2012a-30">[30]</a></sup> Glacial lakes include <a href="/wiki/Proglacial_lake" title="Proglacial lake">proglacial lakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subglacial_lake" title="Subglacial lake">subglacial lakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finger_lake" title="Finger lake">finger lakes</a>, and epishelf lakes. Epishelf lakes are highly stratified lakes in which a layer of freshwater, derived from ice and snow melt, is dammed behind an <a href="/wiki/Ice_shelf" title="Ice shelf">ice shelf</a> that is attached to the coastline. They are mostly found in Antarctica.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VeilletteOthers2008a_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-VeilletteOthers2008a-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fluvial_lakes">Fluvial lakes</span></h3>
<p>Fluvial (or riverine)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-33">[33]</a></sup> lakes are lakes produced by running water. These lakes include <a href="/wiki/Plunge_pool" title="Plunge pool">plunge pool lakes</a>, fluviatile dams and meander lakes.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxbow_lakes">Oxbow lakes</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nowitna_river.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="616" data-file-width="875" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Nowitna_river.jpg/220px-Nowitna_river.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Nowitna_river.jpg/330px-Nowitna_river.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Nowitna_river.jpg/440px-Nowitna_river.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nowitna_river.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Nowitna_River" title="Nowitna River">Nowitna River</a> in Alaska. Two oxbow lakes – a short one at the bottom of the picture and a longer, more curved one at the middle-right.</div></div></div>
<p>The most common type of fluvial lake is a crescent-shaped lake called an <i><a href="/wiki/Oxbow_lake" title="Oxbow lake">oxbow lake</a></i> due to the distinctive curved shape. They can form in river valleys as a result of meandering. The slow-moving river forms a sinuous shape as the outer side of bends are eroded away more rapidly than the inner side. Eventually a horseshoe bend is formed and the river cuts through the narrow neck. This new passage then forms the main passage for the river and the ends of the bend become silted up, thus forming a bow-shaped lake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson1957a_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson1957a-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2003a_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2003a-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-6"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Håkanson2012a_30-6"><a href="#cite_note-Håkanson2012a-30">[30]</a></sup> Their crescent shape gives oxbow lakes a higher perimeter to area ratio than other lake types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fenh_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-fenh-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fluviatile_dams">Fluviatile dams</span></h4>
<p>These form where sediment from a tributary blocks the main river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lateral_lakes">Lateral lakes</span></h4>
<p>These form where sediment from the main river blocks a tributary, usually in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Levee#Natural_examples" title="Levee">levee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Floodplain_lakes">Floodplain lakes</span></h4>
<p>Lakes formed by other processes responsible for <a href="/wiki/Floodplain" title="Floodplain">floodplain</a> basin creation. During high floods they are flushed with river water. There are four types: 1. Confluent floodplain lake, 2. Contrafluent-confluent floodplain lake, 3. Contrafluent floodplain lake, 4. Profundal floodplain lake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solution_lakes">Solution lakes</span></h3>
<p>A solution lake is a lake occupying a basin formed by surface dissolution of bedrock. In areas underlain by soluble bedrock, its solution by precipitation and percolating water commonly produce cavities. These cavities frequently collapse to form <a href="/wiki/Sinkhole" title="Sinkhole">sinkholes</a> that form part of the local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karst_topography" title="Karst topography">karst topography</a>. Where <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> lies near the grounds surface, a sinkhole will be filled water as a solution lake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson1957a_27-6"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson1957a-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-7"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup> If such a lake consists of a large area of standing water that occupies an extensive closed depression in limestone, it is also called a <a href="/wiki/Karst_lake" title="Karst lake">karst lake</a>. Smaller solution lakes that consist of a body of standing water in a closed depression within a karst region are known as <i>karst ponds.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackso1983a_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jackso1983a-36">[36]</a></sup> Limestone caves often contain pools of standing water, which are known as <i><a href="/wiki/Underground_lake" title="Underground lake">underground lakes</a>.</i> Classic examples of solution lakes are abundant in the karst regions at the <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">Dalmatian coast</a> of <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a> and within large parts of <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson1957a_27-7"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson1957a-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Landslide_lakes">Landslide lakes</span></h3>
<p>A landslide lake is created by the <a href="/wiki/Landslide_dam" title="Landslide dam">blockage</a> of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_valley" title="River valley">river valley</a> by either <a href="/wiki/Mudflow" title="Mudflow">mudflows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rockslide" title="Rockslide">rockslides</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Scree" title="Scree">screes</a>. Such lakes are most common in mountainous regions. Although landslide lakes may be large and quite deep, they are typically short-lived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson1957a_27-8"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson1957a-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2003a_28-7"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2003a-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-8"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Håkanson2012a_30-7"><a href="#cite_note-Håkanson2012a-30">[30]</a></sup> An example of a landslide lake is <a href="/wiki/Quake_Lake" title="Quake Lake">Quake Lake</a>, which formed as a result of the <a href="/wiki/1959_Hebgen_Lake_earthquake" title="1959 Hebgen Lake earthquake">1959 Hebgen Lake earthquake</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MyersOthers1964a_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-MyersOthers1964a-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most landslide lakes disappear in the first few months after formation, but a landslide dam can burst suddenly at a later stage and threaten the population downstream when the lake water drains out. In 1911, an earthquake triggered a landslide that blocked a deep valley in the <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Mountains" title="Pamir Mountains">Pamir Mountains</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, forming the <a href="/wiki/Sarez_Lake" title="Sarez Lake">Sarez Lake</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Usoi_Dam" title="Usoi Dam">Usoi Dam</a> at the base of the valley has remained in place for more than 100 years but the terrain below the lake is in danger of a catastrophic flood if the dam were to fail during a future earthquake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tal-y-llyn_Lake" title="Tal-y-llyn Lake">Tal-y-llyn Lake</a> in north <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> is a landslide lake dating back to the last glaciation in Wales some 20000 years ago.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aeolian_lakes">Aeolian lakes</span></h3>
<p>Aeolian lakes are produced by <a href="/wiki/Aeolian_processes" title="Aeolian processes">wind action</a>. These lakes are found mainly in <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> environments, although some aeolian lakes are <a href="/wiki/Relict_(geology)" title="Relict (geology)">relict</a> landforms indicative of arid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleoclimate" title="Paleoclimate">paleoclimates</a>. Aeolian lakes consist of lake basins dammed by wind-blown sand; interdunal lakes that lie between well-oriented <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sand_dune" title="Sand dune">sand dunes</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Blowout_(geomorphology)" title="Blowout (geomorphology)">deflation basins</a> formed by wind action under previously arid paleoenvironments. <a href="/wiki/Moses_Lake" title="Moses Lake">Moses Lake</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>, United States, was originally a shallow natural lake and an example of a lake basin dammed by wind-blown sand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson1957a_27-9"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson1957a-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2003a_28-8"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2003a-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-9"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Håkanson2012a_30-8"><a href="#cite_note-Håkanson2012a-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>China's <a href="/wiki/Badain_Jaran_Desert" title="Badain Jaran Desert">Badain Jaran Desert</a> is a unique landscape of megadunes and elongated interdunal aeolian lakes, particularly concentrated in the southeastern margin of the desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shoreline_lakes">Shoreline lakes</span></h3>
<p>Shoreline lakes are generally lakes created by blockage of estuaries or by the uneven accretion of beach ridges by longshore and other currents. They include maritime coastal lakes, ordinarily in drowned estuaries; lakes enclosed by two tombolos or spits connecting an island to the mainland; lakes cut off from larger lakes by a bar; or lakes divided by the meeting of two spits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson1957a_27-10"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson1957a-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-10"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2003a_28-9"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2003a-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Håkanson2012a_30-9"><a href="#cite_note-Håkanson2012a-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_lakes">Organic lakes</span></h3>
<p>Organic lakes are lakes created by the actions of plants and animals. On the whole they are relatively rare in occurrence and quite small in size. In addition, they typically ephemeral features relative to the other types of lakes. The basins in which organic lakes occur are associated with beaver dams, coral lakes, or dams formed by vegetation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-11"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Håkanson2012a_30-10"><a href="#cite_note-Håkanson2012a-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Peat_lakes">Peat lakes</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">Peat</a> lakes are a form of organic lake. They form where a buildup of partly decomposed plant material in a wet environment leaves the vegetated surface below the <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water table</a> for a sustained period of time. They are often low in nutrients and mildly acidic, with bottom waters low in dissolved oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropogenic_lakes">Anthropogenic lakes</span></h3>
<p>Anthropogenic lakes are artificially created as a result of human activity. They can be formed by the intentional damming of rivers and streams or the subsequent filling of abandoned excavations by either ground water, precipitation, or a combination of both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-12"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Håkanson2012a_30-11"><a href="#cite_note-Håkanson2012a-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Upper_Silesia" title="Upper Silesia">Upper Silesian</a> region of Southern Poland contains an anthropogenic lake district consisting of more than 4,000 water bodies created by human activity. The diverse origins of these lakes include: reservoirs retained by dams, flooded mines, water bodies formed in subsidence basins and hollows, <a href="/wiki/Levee" title="Levee">levee</a> ponds, and residual water bodies following river regulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Meteorite_.28extraterrestrial_impact.29_lakes"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Meteorite_(extraterrestrial_impact)_lakes">Meteorite (extraterrestrial impact) lakes</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater_lake" title="Impact crater lake">Impact crater lake</a></div>
<p>Meteorite lakes, also known as <i>crater lakes</i> (not to be confused with <a href="#Volcanic_lakes">volcanic crater lakes</a>), are created by catastrophic <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">impacts</a> with the Earth by extraterrestrial objects (either <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> or <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson1957a_27-11"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson1957a-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-13"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Håkanson2012a_30-12"><a href="#cite_note-Håkanson2012a-30">[30]</a></sup> Examples of meteorite lakes are <a href="/wiki/Lonar_Lake" title="Lonar Lake">Lonar Lake</a> in India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaloofOther2010a_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-MaloofOther2010a-42">[42]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_El%27gygytgyn" title="Lake El'gygytgyn">Lake El'gygytgyn</a> in northeast Siberia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WennrichOthers2016a_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-WennrichOthers2016a-43">[43]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Pingualuit_crater" title="Pingualuit crater">Pingualuit crater</a> lake in Quebec, Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DesiageOthers2015a_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-DesiageOthers2015a-44">[44]</a></sup> As in the cases of El'gygytgyn and Pingualuit, meteorite lakes can contain unique and scientifically valuable sedimentary deposits associated with long records of paleoclimatic changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WennrichOthers2016a_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-WennrichOthers2016a-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DesiageOthers2015a_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-DesiageOthers2015a-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_classification_methods">Other classification methods</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lakesalaskarange.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1232" data-file-width="1632" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Lakesalaskarange.JPG/220px-Lakesalaskarange.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Lakesalaskarange.JPG/330px-Lakesalaskarange.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Lakesalaskarange.JPG/440px-Lakesalaskarange.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lakesalaskarange.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>These <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kettle_lake" title="Kettle lake">kettle lakes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> were formed by a retreating glacier.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ice_Melting_on_Lake_Balaton.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Ice_Melting_on_Lake_Balaton.jpg/220px-Ice_Melting_on_Lake_Balaton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Ice_Melting_on_Lake_Balaton.jpg/330px-Ice_Melting_on_Lake_Balaton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Ice_Melting_on_Lake_Balaton.jpg/440px-Ice_Melting_on_Lake_Balaton.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ice_Melting_on_Lake_Balaton.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ice melting on <a href="/wiki/Lake_Balaton" title="Lake Balaton">Lake Balaton</a> in Hungary</div></div></div>
<p>In addition to the mode of origin, lakes have been named and classified according to various other important factors such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_stratification" title="Thermal stratification">thermal stratification</a>, oxygen saturation, seasonal variations in lake volume and water level, <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> of the water mass, relative seasonal permanence, degree of outflow, and so on. The names used by the lay public and in the scientific community for different types of lakes are often informally derived from the morphology of the lakes' physical characteristics or other factors. Also, different cultures and regions of the world have their own popular nomenclature.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_thermal_stratification">By thermal stratification</span></h3>
<p>One important method of lake classification is on the basis of thermal stratification, which has a major influence on the animal and plant life inhabiting a lake, and the fate and distribution of dissolved and suspended material in the lake. For example, the thermal stratification, as well as the degree and frequency of mixing, has a strong control over the distribution of oxygen within the lake.
</p><p>Professor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F.-A._Forel" title="F.-A. Forel">F.-A. Forel</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forel1901a_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forel1901a-45">[45]</a></sup> also referred to as the "Father of limnology", was the first scientist to classify lakes according to their thermal stratification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loffler1957a_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loffler1957a-46">[46]</a></sup> His system of classification was later modified and improved upon by <a href="/wiki/G._Evelyn_Hutchinson" title="G. Evelyn Hutchinson">Hutchinson</a> and Löffler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HutchinsonOther1956a_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-HutchinsonOther1956a-47">[47]</a></sup> As the <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of water varies with temperature, with a maximum at +4 degrees Celsius, thermal stratification is an important physical characteristic of a lake that controls the <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">fauna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">flora</a>, sedimentation, chemistry, and other aspects of individual lakes. First, the colder, denser water typically forms a layer near the bottom, which is called the <i><a href="/wiki/Hypolimnion" title="Hypolimnion">hypolimnion</a></i>. Second, normally overlying the hypolimnion is a transition zone known as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metalimnion" title="Metalimnion">metalimnion</a></i>. Finally, overlying the metalimnion is a surface layer of warmer water with a lower density, called the <i><a href="/wiki/Epilimnion" title="Epilimnion">epilimnion</a></i>. This typical stratification sequence can vary widely, depending on the specific lake or the time of year, or a combination of both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-14"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loffler1957a_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Loffler1957a-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HutchinsonOther1956a_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-HutchinsonOther1956a-47">[47]</a></sup> The classification of lakes by thermal stratification presupposes lakes with sufficient depth to form a hypolimnion; accordingly, very shallow lakes are excluded from this classification system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-15"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HutchinsonOther1956a_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-HutchinsonOther1956a-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Based upon their thermal stratification, lakes are classified as either <i><a href="/wiki/Holomictic_lake" title="Holomictic lake">holomictic</a></i>, with a uniform temperature and density from top to bottom at a given time of year, or <i><a href="/wiki/Meromictic_lake" title="Meromictic lake">meromictic</a></i>, with layers of water of different temperature and density that do not intermix. The deepest layer of water in a meromictic lake does not contain any dissolved oxygen so there are no living <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism">aerobic organisms</a>. Consequently, the layers of sediment at the bottom of a meromictic lake remain relatively undisturbed, which allows for the development of <a href="/wiki/Lacustrine_deposits" title="Lacustrine deposits">lacustrine deposits</a>. In a holomictic lake, the uniformity of temperature and density allows the lake waters to completely mix. Based upon thermal stratification and frequency of turnover, holomictic lakes are divided into <a href="/wiki/Amictic_lake" title="Amictic lake">amictic lakes</a>, cold <a href="/wiki/Monomictic_lake" title="Monomictic lake">monomictic lakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dimictic_lake" title="Dimictic lake">dimictic lakes</a>, warm monomictic lakes, <a href="/wiki/Polymictic_lake" title="Polymictic lake">polymictic lakes</a>, and oligomictic lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-16"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HutchinsonOther1956a_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-HutchinsonOther1956a-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Lake stratification does not always result from a variation in density because of thermal gradients. <a href="/wiki/Stratification_(water)" title="Stratification (water)">Stratification</a> can also result from a density variation caused by gradients in salinity. In this case, the hypolimnion and epilimnion are separated not by a thermocline but by a <i><a href="/wiki/Halocline" title="Halocline">halocline</a></i>, which is sometimes referred to as a <i><a href="/wiki/Chemocline" title="Chemocline">chemocline</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HåkansonOthers1983a_29-17"><a href="#cite_note-HåkansonOthers1983a-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HutchinsonOther1956a_47-4"><a href="#cite_note-HutchinsonOther1956a-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p><span id="Seasonal_lakes"></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_seasonal_variations_in_water_level_and_volume">By seasonal variations in water level and volume</span></h3>
<p>Lakes are informally classified and named according to the seasonal variation in their lake level and volume. Some of the names include:
</p>
<ul><li>Ephemeral lake is a short-lived lake or pond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gangstad1979a_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gangstad1979a-48">[48]</a></sup> If it fills with water and dries up (disappears) seasonally it is known as an <i>intermittent lake</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poehls2009a_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poehls2009a-49">[49]</a></sup> They often fill <a href="/wiki/Polje" title="Polje">poljes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dry_lake" title="Dry lake">Dry lake</a> is a popular name for an ephemeral lake that contains water only intermediately at irregular and infrequent intervals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackso1983a_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jackso1983a-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LastOthers2001a_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-LastOthers2001a-51">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li>Perennial lake is a lake that has water in its basin throughout the year and is not subject to extreme fluctuations in level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackso1983a_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jackso1983a-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gangstad1979a_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gangstad1979a-48">[48]</a></sup></li>
<li>Playa lake is a typically shallow, intermittent lake that covers or occupies a playa either in wet seasons or in especially wet years but subsequently drying up in an arid or semiarid region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackso1983a_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Jackso1983a-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LastOthers2001a_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-LastOthers2001a-51">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vlei" title="Vlei">Vlei</a> is a name used in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> for a shallow lake which varies considerably in level with the seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theal1877a_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-theal1877a-52">[52]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="By_water_chemistry">By water chemistry</span></h3>
<p>Lakes may be informally classified and named according to the general chemistry of their water mass. Using this classification method, the lake types include:
</p>
<ul><li>An <i>acid lake</i> contains water with a below-neutral <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of less than 6.5. A lake is considered to be highly acidic if its pH drops below 5.5, leading to biological consequences. Such lakes include: acidic <i>pit lakes</i> occupying abandoned mines and excavations; naturally acidic lakes of <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic</a> landscapes; <a href="/wiki/Raised_bog" title="Raised bog">peat bogs</a> in northern regions; <i>crater lakes</i> of active and dormant volcanoes; and lakes acidified by <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GellerOthers2013a_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-GellerOthers2013a-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PatrickOthers1981a_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-PatrickOthers1981a-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RouwetOthers2015a_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-RouwetOthers2015a-55">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li>A <i>salt lake</i>, also known as a <i>saline lake</i> or <i>brine lake</i>, is an inland body of water situated in an arid or semiarid region, with no outlet to the sea, containing a high concentration of dissolved neutral salts (principally <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a>). Examples include the Great Salt Lake in Utah, and the Dead Sea in southwestern Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackso1983a_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-Jackso1983a-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LastOthers2001a_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-LastOthers2001a-51">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li>An <i><a href="/wiki/Alkali_sink" title="Alkali sink">alkali sink</a></i>, also known as an <i>alkali flat</i> or <i>salt flat</i>, is a shallow saline feature that can be found in low-lying areas of arid regions and in groundwater discharge zones. These features are typically classified as <i>dry lakes</i>, or <i>playas</i>, because they are periodically flooded by rain or flood events and then dry up during drier intervals, leaving accumulations of brines and evaporitic minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackso1983a_36-5"><a href="#cite_note-Jackso1983a-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LastOthers2001a_51-3"><a href="#cite_note-LastOthers2001a-51">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li>A <i><a href="/wiki/Salt_pan_(geology)" title="Salt pan (geology)">salt pan</a></i> is a small shallow natural depression in which water accumulates and evaporates, leaving a salt deposit, or the shallow lake of <a href="/wiki/Brackish_water" title="Brackish water">brackish water</a> that occupies a salt pan. (The term "salt pan" comes from <a href="/wiki/Open-pan_salt_making" title="Open-pan salt making">open-pan salt making</a>, a method of extracting salt from brine using large open pans.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackso1983a_36-6"><a href="#cite_note-Jackso1983a-36">[36]</a></sup></li>
<li>A <i>saline pan</i> is another name for an <a href="/wiki/Ephemeral_acid_saline_lake" title="Ephemeral acid saline lake">ephemeral acid saline lake</a> which precipitates a bottom crust that is subsequently modified during subaerial exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackso1983a_36-7"><a href="#cite_note-Jackso1983a-36">[36]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composed_of_other_liquids">Composed of other liquids</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lava_lake" title="Lava lake">Lava lake</a> is a large volume of molten lava, usually basaltic, contained in a volcanic vent, crater, or broad depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WithamOthers2006a_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-WithamOthers2006a-56">[56]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lakes_of_Titan" title="Lakes of Titan">Hydrocarbon lakes</a> are bodies of liquid <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> that occupy depressions on the surface of <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>. They were detected by the Cassini–Huygens space probe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MastrogiuseppeOthers2014a_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-MastrogiuseppeOthers2014a-57">[57]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleolakes">Paleolakes</span></h2>
<p>A  <b>paleolake</b> (also <b>palaeolake</b>) is a lake that existed in the past when hydrological conditions were different.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2003a_28-10"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2003a-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Quaternary" title="Quaternary">Quaternary</a> paleolakes can often be identified on the basis of <a href="/wiki/Relict_(geology)" title="Relict (geology)">relict</a> lacustrine landforms, such as relict lake plains and coastal landforms that form recognizable relict shorelines called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleoshorelines" title="Paleoshorelines">paleoshorelines</a>.</i> Paleolakes can also be recognized by characteristic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sedimentary" title="Sedimentary">sedimentary</a> deposits that accumulated in them and any <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossils</a> that might be contained in these sediments. The paleoshorelines and sedimentary deposits of paleolakes provide evidence for prehistoric hydrological changes during the times that they existed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen2003a_28-11"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2003a-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goudie2008a_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goudie2008a-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two types of paleolake:
</p>
<ul><li>A <a href="/wiki/Category:Former_lakes" title="Category:Former lakes">former lake</a> is a paleolake that no longer exists. Such lakes include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric" title="Prehistoric">prehistoric</a> lakes and those that have permanently dried up, often as the result of either <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> or human intervention. An example of a former lake is <a href="/wiki/Owens_Lake" title="Owens Lake">Owens Lake</a> in California, United States. Former lakes are a common feature of the <a href="/wiki/Basin_and_Range_Province" title="Basin and Range Province">Basin and Range</a> area of southwestern North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manivanan2008a_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manivanan2008a-59">[59]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="anchor" id="Shrunken_lake"></span>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shrunken_lake" title="Shrunken lake">shrunken lake</a> is a paleolake that still exists but has considerably decreased in size over geological time. An example of a shrunken lake is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Agassiz" title="Lake Agassiz">Lake Agassiz</a>, which once covered much of central North America. Two notable remnants of Lake Agassiz are <a href="/wiki/Lake_Winnipeg" title="Lake Winnipeg">Lake Winnipeg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Winnipegosis" title="Lake Winnipegosis">Lake Winnipegosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manivanan2008a_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Manivanan2008a-59">[59]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Paleolakes are of scientific and economic importance. For example, Quaternary paleolakes in semidesert basins are important for two reasons: they played an extremely significant, if transient, role in shaping the floors and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piedmont_(geography)" title="Piedmont (geography)">piedmonts</a> of many basins; and their sediments contain enormous quantities of geologic and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleontologic" title="Paleontologic">paleontologic</a> information concerning past environments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Currey1990a_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Currey1990a-60">[60]</a></sup> In addition, the organic-rich deposits of pre-Quaternary paleolakes are important either for the thick deposits of <a href="/wiki/Oil_shale" title="Oil shale">oil shale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas" title="Shale gas">shale gas</a> contained in them, or as source rocks of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>. Although of significantly less economic importance, strata deposited along the shore of paleolakes sometimes contain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_seam" title="Coal seam">coal seams</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gierlowski-KordeschOthers2000a_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gierlowski-KordeschOthers2000a-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchnurrenbergerOthers2003a_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchnurrenbergerOthers2003a-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output
