<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:China_Hangzhou_Westlake-8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/China_Hangzhou_Westlake-8.jpg/220px-China_Hangzhou_Westlake-8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/China_Hangzhou_Westlake-8.jpg/330px-China_Hangzhou_Westlake-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/China_Hangzhou_Westlake-8.jpg/440px-China_Hangzhou_Westlake-8.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:China_Hangzhou_Westlake-8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lakes can have significant cultural importance. The <a href="/wiki/West_Lake" title="West Lake">West Lake</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a> has inspired romantic poets throughout the ages, and has been an important influence on garden designs in China, Japan and Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg/220px-Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg/330px-Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg/440px-Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lake_mapourika_NZ.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lake_Mapourika" title="Lake Mapourika">Lake Mapourika</a>, New Zealand</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E4%B9%9D%E5%AF%A8%E6%BA%9D-%E4%BA%94%E8%8A%B1%E6%B5%B7_Jiuzhaigou_Five_Flower_Lake.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/%E4%B9%9D%E5%AF%A8%E6%BA%9D-%E4%BA%94%E8%8A%B1%E6%B5%B7_Jiuzhaigou_Five_Flower_Lake.jpg/220px-%E4%B9%9D%E5%AF%A8%E6%BA%9D-%E4%BA%94%E8%8A%B1%E6%B5%B7_Jiuzhaigou_Five_Flower_Lake.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/%E4%B9%9D%E5%AF%A8%E6%BA%9D-%E4%BA%94%E8%8A%B1%E6%B5%B7_Jiuzhaigou_Five_Flower_Lake.jpg/330px-%E4%B9%9D%E5%AF%A8%E6%BA%9D-%E4%BA%94%E8%8A%B1%E6%B5%B7_Jiuzhaigou_Five_Flower_Lake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/%E4%B9%9D%E5%AF%A8%E6%BA%9D-%E4%BA%94%E8%8A%B1%E6%B5%B7_Jiuzhaigou_Five_Flower_Lake.jpg/440px-%E4%B9%9D%E5%AF%A8%E6%BA%9D-%E4%BA%94%E8%8A%B1%E6%B5%B7_Jiuzhaigou_Five_Flower_Lake.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E4%B9%9D%E5%AF%A8%E6%BA%9D-%E4%BA%94%E8%8A%B1%E6%B5%B7_Jiuzhaigou_Five_Flower_Lake.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Five Flower Lake in <a href="/wiki/Jiuzhaigou" title="Jiuzhaigou">Jiuzhaigou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a></div></div></div>
<p>Lakes have numerous features in addition to lake type, such as <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> (also known as catchment area), inflow and outflow, <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrient</a> content, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissolved_oxygen" title="Dissolved oxygen">dissolved oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">pollutants</a>, <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sedimentation</a>.
</p><p>Changes in the level of a lake are controlled by the difference between the input and output compared to the total volume of the lake. Significant input sources are precipitation onto the lake, runoff carried by streams and channels from the lake's <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">catchment</a> area, <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> channels and aquifers, and artificial sources from outside the catchment area. Output sources are evaporation from the lake, surface and groundwater flows, and any extraction of lake water by humans. As climate conditions and human water requirements vary, these will create fluctuations in the lake level.
</p><p>Lakes can be also <a href="/wiki/Trophic_state_index" title="Trophic state index">categorized</a> on the basis of their richness in nutrients, which typically affect plant growth. Nutrient-poor lakes are said to be <i><a href="/wiki/Oligotroph" title="Oligotroph">oligotrophic</a></i> and are generally clear, having a low concentration of plant life. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesotrophic_lake" title="Mesotrophic lake">Mesotrophic lakes</a></i> have good clarity and an average level of nutrients. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eutrophic" title="Eutrophic">Eutrophic</a></i> lakes are enriched with nutrients, resulting in good plant growth and possible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algal_blooms" title="Algal blooms">algal blooms</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">Hypertrophic</a></i> lakes are bodies of water that have been excessively enriched with nutrients. These lakes typically have poor clarity and are subject to devastating algal blooms. Lakes typically reach this condition due to human activities, such as heavy use of fertilizers in the lake catchment area. Such lakes are of little use to humans and have a poor <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> due to decreased dissolved oxygen.
</p><p>Due to the unusual relationship between water's <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> and its <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>, lakes form layers called <a href="/wiki/Thermocline" title="Thermocline">thermoclines</a>, layers of drastically varying temperature relative to depth. Fresh water is most dense at about 4 degrees Celsius (39.2 °F) at sea level. When the temperature of the water at the surface of a lake reaches the same temperature as deeper water, as it does during the cooler months in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">temperate</a> climates, the water in the lake can mix, bringing oxygen-starved water up from the depths and bringing oxygen down to decomposing sediments. Deep temperate lakes can maintain a reservoir of cold water year-round, which allows some cities to tap that reservoir for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_lake_water_cooling" title="Deep lake water cooling">deep lake water cooling</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yaylyu.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Yaylyu.JPG/220px-Yaylyu.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Yaylyu.JPG/330px-Yaylyu.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Yaylyu.JPG/440px-Yaylyu.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yaylyu.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lake_Teletskoye" title="Lake Teletskoye">Lake Teletskoye</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Since the surface water of deep <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical" title="Tropical">tropical</a> lakes never reaches the temperature of maximum density, there is no process that makes the water mix. The deeper layer becomes oxygen starved and can become saturated with carbon dioxide, or other gases such as sulfur dioxide if there is even a trace of <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic activity</a>. Exceptional events, such as earthquakes or landslides, can cause mixing which rapidly brings the deep layers up to the surface and release a vast cloud of gas which lay trapped in solution in the colder water at the bottom of the lake. This is called a <a href="/wiki/Limnic_eruption" title="Limnic eruption">limnic eruption</a>. An example is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Nyos#The_1986_disaster" title="Lake Nyos">the disaster at Lake Nyos</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>. The amount of gas that can be dissolved in water is directly related to pressure. As deep water surfaces, the pressure drops and a vast amount of gas comes out of solution. Under these circumstances carbon dioxide is hazardous because it is heavier than air and displaces it, so it may flow down a river valley to human settlements and cause mass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asphyxiation" title="Asphyxiation">asphyxiation</a>.
</p><p>The material at the bottom of a lake, or <i>lake bed</i>, may be composed of a wide variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inorganic" title="Inorganic">inorganics</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sand" title="Sand">sand</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_material" title="Organic material">organic material</a>, such as decaying plant or animal matter. The composition of the lake bed has a significant impact on the flora and fauna found within the lake's environs by contributing to the amounts and the types of nutrients available.
</p><p>A paired (black and white) layer of the varved lake sediments correspond to a year. During winter, when organisms die, carbon is deposited down, resulting to a black layer. At the same year, during summer, only few organic materials are deposited, resulting to a white layer at the lake bed. These are commonly used to track past paleontological events.
</p><p>Natural lakes provide a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrocosm_and_microcosm" title="Macrocosm and microcosm">microcosm</a> of living and nonliving elements that are relatively independent of their surrounding environments. Therefore, lake organisms can often be studied in isolation from the lake's surroundings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Limnology">Limnology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Limnology" title="Limnology">Limnology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_ecosystem" title="Lake ecosystem">Lake ecosystem</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_aeration" title="Lake aeration">Lake aeration</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lura_Liqeni_i_Lul%C3%ABve.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Lura_Liqeni_i_Lul%C3%ABve.JPG/220px-Lura_Liqeni_i_Lul%C3%ABve.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Lura_Liqeni_i_Lul%C3%ABve.JPG/330px-Lura_Liqeni_i_Lul%C3%ABve.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Lura_Liqeni_i_Lul%C3%ABve.JPG/440px-Lura_Liqeni_i_Lul%C3%ABve.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lura_Liqeni_i_Lul%C3%ABve.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lake of Flowers (Liqeni i Lulëve), one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lur%C3%AB_National_Park" title="Lurë National Park">Lurë Mountains</a> glacial lakes, <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Limnology" title="Limnology">Limnology</a> is the study of inland bodies of water and related ecosystems. Limnology divides lakes into three zones: the <i><a href="/wiki/Littoral_zone" title="Littoral zone">littoral zone</a></i>, a sloped area close to land; the <i><a href="/wiki/Photic_zone" title="Photic zone">photic</a></i> or <i>open-water zone</i>, where sunlight is abundant; and the deep-water <i><a href="/wiki/Profundal_zone" title="Profundal zone">profundal</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Benthic_zone" title="Benthic zone">benthic zone</a></i>, where little sunlight can reach. The depth to which light can penetrate depends on the <a href="/wiki/Turbidity" title="Turbidity">turbidity</a> of the water, which is determined by the density and size of suspended <a href="/wiki/Particle_(ecology)" title="Particle (ecology)">particles</a>. A particle will be in <a href="/wiki/Suspension_(chemistry)" title="Suspension (chemistry)">suspension</a> if its weight is less than the random turbidity <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">forces</a> acting upon it. These particles can be sedimentary or <a href="/wiki/Biotic_material" title="Biotic material">biological</a> in origin (including <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Detritus" title="Detritus">detritus</a>) and are responsible for the color of the water. Decaying plant matter, for instance, may account for a yellow or brown color, while algae may cause a greenish coloration. In very shallow water bodies, iron oxides make the water reddish brown. Bottom-dwelling <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detritivorous" title="Detritivorous">detritivorous</a> fish stir the mud in search of food and can be the cause of turbid waters. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piscivorous" title="Piscivorous">Piscivorous</a> fish contribute to turbidity by eating plant-eating (<a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">planktonivorous</a>) fish, thus increasing the amount of algae (see aquatic <a href="/wiki/Trophic_cascade" title="Trophic cascade">trophic cascade</a>).
</p><p>The light depth or transparency is measured using a <i><a href="/wiki/Secchi_disk" title="Secchi disk">Secchi disk</a></i>, a 20-cm (8 in) disk with alternating white and black <a href="/wiki/Circular_sector" title="Circular sector">quadrants</a>. The depth at which the disk is no longer visible is the <i>Secchi depth</i>, a measure of transparency. The Secchi disk is commonly used to test for eutrophication. For a detailed look at these processes, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lentic_ecosystems" title="Lentic ecosystems">lentic ecosystems</a>.
</p><p>A lake moderates the surrounding region's temperature and <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> because water has a very high <a href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">specific heat capacity</a> (4,186 J·kg<sup>−1</sup>·K<sup>−1</sup>). In the daytime a lake can cool the land beside it with local winds, resulting in a <a href="/wiki/Sea_breeze" title="Sea breeze">sea breeze</a>; in the night it can warm it with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_breeze" title="Land breeze">land breeze</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_properties">Biological properties</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lake_zones_(left)_and_algal_communities_(right).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="927" data-file-width="1892" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Lake_zones_%28left%29_and_algal_communities_%28right%29.jpg/330px-Lake_zones_%28left%29_and_algal_communities_%28right%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Lake_zones_%28left%29_and_algal_communities_%28right%29.jpg/495px-Lake_zones_%28left%29_and_algal_communities_%28right%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Lake_zones_%28left%29_and_algal_communities_%28right%29.jpg/660px-Lake_zones_%28left%29_and_algal_communities_%28right%29.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lake_zones_(left)_and_algal_communities_(right).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cross sectional diagram of limnological lake zones (left) and algal community types (right)</div></div></div>
<p>Lake zones:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Epilittoral</i>: The zone that is entirely above the lake's normal water level and never submerged by lake water</li>
<li><i>Littoral</i>: The zone that encompasses the small area above the normal water level (which is sometimes submerged when the lake's water level increases), reaching to the deepest part of the lake that still allows for submerged <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_plant" title="Aquatic plant">macrophytic</a> growth</li>
<li><i>Littoriprofundal</i>: Transition zone commonly aligned with stratified lakes' metalimnions – too deep for macrophytes but includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photosynthetic" title="Photosynthetic">photosynthetic</a> algae and bacteria</li>
<li><i>Profundal</i>: Sedimentary zone containing no vegetation</li></ul>
<p>Algal community types:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Epipelic</i>: Algae that grow on sediments</li>
<li><i>Epilithic</i>: Algae that grow on rocks</li>
<li><i>Epipsammic</i>: Algae that grow on (or within) sand</li>
<li><i>Epiphytic</i>: Algae that grow on macrophytes</li>
<li><i>Epizooic</i>: Algae that grow on living animals</li>
<li><i>Metaphyton</i>: Algae present in the littoral zone, not in a state of suspension nor attached to a substratum (such as a macrophyte)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Circulation">Circulation</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Empty_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section is empty.</b>  You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Lake&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2020</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora_and_fauna">Flora and fauna</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Empty_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section is empty.</b>  You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Lake&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2020</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Disappearance">Disappearance</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lake_Badwater,_Death_Valley,_2005.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Lake_Badwater%2C_Death_Valley%2C_2005.jpg/220px-Lake_Badwater%2C_Death_Valley%2C_2005.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Lake_Badwater%2C_Death_Valley%2C_2005.jpg/330px-Lake_Badwater%2C_Death_Valley%2C_2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Lake_Badwater%2C_Death_Valley%2C_2005.jpg/440px-Lake_Badwater%2C_Death_Valley%2C_2005.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lake_Badwater,_Death_Valley,_2005.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ephemeral" title="Ephemeral">Ephemeral</a> 'Lake Badwater', a lake only noted after heavy winter and spring rainfall, <a href="/wiki/Badwater_Basin" title="Badwater Basin">Badwater Basin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Death_Valley_National_Park" title="Death Valley National Park">Death Valley National Park</a>, 9 February 2005. <a href="/wiki/Landsat_5" title="Landsat 5">Landsat 5</a> satellite photo</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Badwater_tm5_2007046.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Badwater_tm5_2007046.jpg/220px-Badwater_tm5_2007046.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Badwater_tm5_2007046.jpg/330px-Badwater_tm5_2007046.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Badwater_tm5_2007046.jpg/440px-Badwater_tm5_2007046.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Badwater_tm5_2007046.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Badwater_Basin" title="Badwater Basin">Badwater Basin</a> <a href="/wiki/Dry_lake" title="Dry lake">dry lake</a>, 15 February 2007. <a href="/wiki/Landsat_5" title="Landsat 5">Landsat 5</a> satellite photo</div></div></div>
<p>The lake may be infilled with deposited sediment and gradually become a <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetland</a> such as a <a href="/wiki/Swamp" title="Swamp">swamp</a> or <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marsh</a>.  Large water plants, typically <a href="/wiki/Phragmites" title="Phragmites">reeds</a>, accelerate this closing process significantly because they partially decompose to form peat soils that fill the shallows. Conversely, peat soils in a marsh can naturally burn and reverse this process to recreate a shallow lake resulting in a dynamic equilibrium between marsh and lake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> This is significant since wildfire has been largely suppressed in the developed world over the past century. This has artificially converted many shallow lakes into emergent marshes. Turbid lakes and lakes with many plant-eating fish tend to disappear more slowly. A "disappearing" lake (barely noticeable on a human timescale) typically has extensive plant mats at the water's edge. These become a new habitat for other plants, like <a href="/wiki/Sphagnum" title="Sphagnum">peat moss</a> when conditions are right, and animals, many of which are very rare. Gradually, the lake closes and young <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a> may form, forming a <a href="/wiki/Fen" title="Fen">fen</a>. In lowland river valleys where a river can <a href="/wiki/Meander" title="Meander">meander</a>, the presence of peat is explained by the infilling of historical <a href="/wiki/Oxbow_lake" title="Oxbow lake">oxbow lakes</a>. In the final stages of <a href="/wiki/Ecological_succession" title="Ecological succession">succession</a>, trees can grow in, eventually turning the wetland into a forest.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Intermittent_lake"></span><span class="anchor" id="Ephemeral_lake"></span><span class="anchor" id="Seasonal_lake"></span><span class="anchor" id="How_lakes_disappear"></span>
Some lakes can disappear seasonally. These are called <b>intermittent lakes</b>, <b>ephemeral lakes</b>, or <b>seasonal lakes</b> and can be found in <a href="/wiki/Karst" title="Karst">karstic terrain</a>. A prime example of an intermittent lake is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Cerknica" title="Lake Cerknica">Lake Cerknica</a> in <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lag_Prau_Pulte" title="Lag Prau Pulte">Lag Prau Pulte</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graub%C3%BCnden" title="Graubünden">Graubünden</a>. Other intermittent lakes are only the result of above-average precipitation in a closed, or <a href="/wiki/Endorheic_basin" title="Endorheic basin">endorheic basin</a>, usually filling dry lake beds. This can occur in some of the driest places on earth, like <a href="/wiki/Death_Valley" title="Death Valley">Death Valley</a>. This occurred in the spring of 2005, after unusually heavy rains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The lake did not last into the summer, and was quickly evaporated (see photos to right). A more commonly filled lake of this type is <a href="/wiki/Sevier_Lake" title="Sevier Lake">Sevier Lake</a> of west-central <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>.
</p><p>Sometimes a lake will disappear quickly. On 3 June 2005, in <a href="/wiki/Nizhny_Novgorod_Oblast" title="Nizhny Novgorod Oblast">Nizhny Novgorod Oblast</a>, Russia, a lake called <a href="/wiki/Lake_Beloye_(Nizhny_Novgorod_Oblast)" title="Lake Beloye (Nizhny Novgorod Oblast)">Lake Beloye</a> vanished in a matter of minutes. News sources reported that government officials theorized that this strange phenomenon may have been caused by a shift in the soil underneath the lake that allowed its water to drain through channels leading to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oka_River" title="Oka River">Oka River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>The presence of ground permafrost is important to the persistence of some lakes. Thawing permafrost may explain the shrinking or disappearance of hundreds of large Arctic lakes across western Siberia. The idea here is that rising air and soil temperatures thaw permafrost, allowing the lakes to drain away into the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some lakes disappear because of human development factors. The shrinking <a href="/wiki/Aral_Sea" title="Aral Sea">Aral Sea</a> is described as being "murdered" by the diversion for irrigation of the rivers feeding it.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prairie_Lake" title="Prairie Lake">Prairie Lake</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraterrestrial_lakes">Extraterrestrial lakes</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Lakes_of_Titan" title="Lakes of Titan">Lakes of Titan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1900" data-file-width="2348" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg/230px-PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg/345px-PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg/460px-PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA10008_Seas_and_Lakes_on_Titan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan's</a> north polar hydrocarbon <a href="/wiki/Lakes_of_Titan" title="Lakes of Titan">seas and lakes</a>, as seen in a false-color <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini</a></i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_aperture_radar" title="Synthetic aperture radar">synthetic aperture radar</a> mosaic</div></div></div>
<p>Only one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_body" title="Astronomical body">astronomical body</a> other than Earth is known to harbor large lakes: Saturn's largest moon, <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>. Photographs and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectroscopic_analysis" title="Spectroscopic analysis">spectroscopic analysis</a> by the <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">Cassini–Huygens</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> show liquid ethane on the surface, which is thought to be mixed with liquid methane. The largest lake on Titan is <a href="/wiki/Kraken_Mare" title="Kraken Mare">Kraken Mare</a> which, at an estimated 400,000 km<sup>2</sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> is roughly five times the size of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Superior" title="Lake Superior">Lake Superior</a> (~80,000 km<sup>2</sup>) and nearly the size of all five <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> of North America combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The second largest Titanean lake, <a href="/wiki/Ligeia_Mare" title="Ligeia Mare">Ligeia Mare</a>, is almost twice the size of Lake Superior, at an estimated 150,000 km<sup>2</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jupiter's large moon <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a> is volcanically active, leading to the accumulation of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> deposits on the surface. Some photographs taken during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo_spacecraft" title="Galileo spacecraft"><i>Galileo</i></a> mission appear to show lakes of liquid sulfur in volcanic caldera, though these are more analogous to lakes of lava than of water on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The planet <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> has only one confirmed lake which is underground and near the south pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Although the surface of Mars is too cold and has too little <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a> to permit permanent surface water, geologic evidence appears to confirm that <a href="/wiki/Ancient_lake" title="Ancient lake">ancient lakes</a> once formed on the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are dark basaltic plains on the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, similar to <a href="/wiki/Lunar_mare" title="Lunar mare">lunar maria</a> but smaller, which are called <i>lacus</i> (singular <i>lacus</i>, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> for "lake") because they were thought by early astronomers to be lakes of water.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_lakes_on_Earth">Notable lakes on Earth</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caspian_Sea_from_orbit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5600" data-file-width="4400" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Caspian_Sea_from_orbit.jpg/220px-Caspian_Sea_from_orbit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Caspian_Sea_from_orbit.jpg/330px-Caspian_Sea_from_orbit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Caspian_Sea_from_orbit.jpg/440px-Caspian_Sea_from_orbit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caspian_Sea_from_orbit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> is either the world's largest lake or a full-fledged sea<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caspian_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caspian-77">[note 1]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roundtanglelake.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1116" data-file-width="1479" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Roundtanglelake.JPG/220px-Roundtanglelake.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Roundtanglelake.JPG/330px-Roundtanglelake.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Roundtanglelake.JPG/440px-Roundtanglelake.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roundtanglelake.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Round Tangle Lake, one of the <a href="/wiki/Tangle_Lakes" title="Tangle Lakes">Tangle Lakes</a>, 2,864 feet (873 m) above sea level in <a href="/wiki/Interior_Alaska" title="Interior Alaska">interior Alaska</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li>The <b>largest lake by surface area</b> is <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>, which is despite its name considered as a lake from the point of view of geography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> Its surface area is 143,000 sq. mi./371,000 km<sup>2</sup>.</li>
<li>The second largest lake by surface area, and the <b>largest freshwater lake by surface area</b>, is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan-Huron" title="Lake Michigan-Huron">Lake Michigan-Huron</a>, which is hydrologically a single lake. Its surface area is 45,300 sq. mi./117,400 km<sup>2</sup>. For those who consider Lake Michigan-Huron to be separate lakes, and Caspian Sea to be a <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Superior" title="Lake Superior">Lake Superior</a> would be the largest lake at 82,100 km<sup>2</sup> (31,700 square miles)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a> is the <b>deepest</b> lake in the world, located in <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, with a bottom at 1,637 metres (5,371 ft). Its <b>mean depth</b> is also the greatest in the world (749 metres (2,457 ft)).<br/>It is also the world's <b>largest freshwater lake by volume</b> (23,600 cubic kilometres (5,700 cu mi), but much smaller than the Caspian Sea at 78,200 cubic kilometres (18,800 cu mi)), and the second longest (about 630 kilometres (390 mi) from tip to tip).</li>
<li>The world's <b><a href="/wiki/Ancient_lake" title="Ancient lake">oldest lake</a></b> is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a>, followed by <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>. <a href="/wiki/Lake_Maracaibo" title="Lake Maracaibo">Lake Maracaibo</a> is considered by some to be the second-oldest lake on Earth, but since it lies at <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a> and nowadays is a contiguous body of water with the sea, others consider that it has turned into a small <a href="/wiki/Bay" title="Bay">bay</a>.</li>
<li>The <b>longest</b> lake is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>, with a length of about 660 kilometres (410 mi) (measured along the lake's center line).<br/>It is also the third largest by volume, the second oldest, and the second deepest (1,470 metres (4,820 ft)) in the world, after Lake Baikal.</li>
<li>The world's <b>highest</b> lake, if size is not a criterion, may be the crater lake of <a href="/wiki/Ojos_del_Salado" title="Ojos del Salado">Ojos del Salado</a>, at 6,390 metres (20,965 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup></li>
<li>The highest large (greater than 250 square kilometres (97 sq mi)) lake in the world is the 290 square kilometres (110 sq mi)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Lake_Puma_Yumco" title="Lake Puma Yumco">Pumoyong Tso</a> (Pumuoyong Tso<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>), in the <a href="/wiki/Tibet_Autonomous_Region" title="Tibet Autonomous Region">Tibet Autonomous Region</a> of China, at <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1073938472">.mw-parser-output .geo-default,.mw-parser-output .geo-dms,.mw-parser-output .geo-dec{display:inline}.mw-parser-output .geo-nondefault,.mw-parser-output .geo-multi-punct{display:none}.mw-parser-output .longitude,.mw-parser-output .latitude{white-space:nowrap}</style><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Lake&amp;params=28_34_N_90_24_E_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">28°34′N</span> <span class="longitude">90°24′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">28.567°N 90.400°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">28.567; 90.400</span></span></span></a></span>, 5,018 metres (16,463 ft) above sea level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li>The world's <b>highest</b> commercially navigable lake is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Titicaca" title="Lake Titicaca">Lake Titicaca</a> in <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a> at 3,812 m (12,507 ft). It is also the largest lake in South America.</li>
<li>The world's <b>lowest</b> lake is the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">Dead Sea</a>, bordered by <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a> to the east and <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and <a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">Palestine</a> to the west, at 418 metres (1,371 ft) below sea level. It is also one of the lakes with highest <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a> concentration.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan%E2%80%93Huron" title="Lake Michigan–Huron">Lake Michigan–Huron</a> has the <b>longest lake coastline</b> in the world: about 5,250 kilometres (3,260 mi), excluding the coastline of its many inner islands. Even if it is considered two lakes, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Huron" title="Lake Huron">Lake Huron</a> alone would still have the longest coastline in the world at 2,980 kilometres (1,850 mi).</li>
<li>The largest island in a lake is <a href="/wiki/Manitoulin_Island" title="Manitoulin Island">Manitoulin Island</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan%E2%80%93Huron" title="Lake Michigan–Huron">Lake Michigan-Huron</a>, with a surface area of 2,766 square kilometres (1,068 sq mi). <a href="/wiki/Lake_Manitou" title="Lake Manitou">Lake Manitou</a>, on Manitoulin Island, is the largest lake on an island in a lake.</li>
<li>The largest lake on an island is <a href="/wiki/Nettilling_Lake" title="Nettilling Lake">Nettilling Lake</a> on <a href="/wiki/Baffin_Island" title="Baffin Island">Baffin Island</a>, with an area of 5,542 square kilometres (2,140 sq mi) and a maximum length of 123 kilometres (76 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup></li>
<li>The largest lake in the world that drains naturally in two directions is <a href="/wiki/Wollaston_Lake" title="Wollaston Lake">Wollaston Lake</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lake_Toba" title="Lake Toba">Lake Toba</a> on the island of <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a> is in what is probably the largest resurgent <a href="/wiki/Caldera" title="Caldera">caldera</a> on Earth.</li>
<li>The largest lake completely within the boundaries of a single city is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Wanapitei" title="Lake Wanapitei">Lake Wanapitei</a> in the city of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Sudbury" title="Greater Sudbury">Sudbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, Canada. Before the current city boundaries came into effect in 2001, this status was held by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Ramsey" title="Lake Ramsey">Lake Ramsey</a>, also in Sudbury.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lake_Enriquillo" title="Lake Enriquillo">Lake Enriquillo</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a> is the only saltwater lake in the world inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lake_Bernard" title="Lake Bernard">Lake Bernard</a>, Ontario, Canada, claims to be the largest lake in the world with no islands.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Saimaa" title="Lake Saimaa">Lake Saimaa</a> in both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Savonia" title="South Savonia">South Savonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Karelia" title="South Karelia">South Karelia</a>, Finland, forms the much larger Saimaa basin, which have more shorelines per unit of area than anywhere else in the world, with the total length being nearly 15,000 kilometres (9,300 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup></li>
<li>The largest lake in one country is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan" title="Lake Michigan">Lake Michigan</a>, in the United States. However, it is sometimes considered part of Lake Michigan-Huron, making the record go to <a href="/wiki/Great_Bear_Lake" title="Great Bear Lake">Great Bear Lake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territories" title="Northwest Territories">Northwest Territories</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, the largest lake within one jurisdiction.</li>
<li>The <b>largest lake on an island in a lake on an island</b> is Crater Lake on Vulcano Island in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Taal" title="Lake Taal">Lake Taal</a> on the island of <a href="/wiki/Luzon" title="Luzon">Luzon</a>, The <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>.</li>
<li>The <b>northernmost named lake</b> on Earth is <a href="/wiki/Upper_Dumbell_Lake" title="Upper Dumbell Lake">Upper Dumbell Lake</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Qikiqtaaluk_Region" title="Qikiqtaaluk Region">Qikiqtaaluk Region</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> at a latitude of 82°28'N. It is 5.2 kilometres (3.2 mi) southwest of <a href="/wiki/Alert,_Nunavut" title="Alert, Nunavut">Alert</a>, the northernmost settlement in the world. There are also several small lakes north of Upper Dumbell Lake, but they are all unnamed and only appear on very detailed maps.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Largest_by_continent">Largest by continent</span></h3>
<p>The largest lakes (surface area) by <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continent</a> are:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Australia</b> – <a href="/wiki/Lake_Eyre" title="Lake Eyre">Lake Eyre</a> (salt lake)</li>
<li><b>Africa</b> – <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a>, also the third-largest freshwater lake on Earth. It is one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_of_Africa" title="Great Lakes of Africa">Great Lakes of Africa</a>.</li>
<li><b>Antarctica</b> – <a href="/wiki/Lake_Vostok" title="Lake Vostok">Lake Vostok</a> (subglacial)</li>
<li><b>Asia</b> – <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a> (if the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> is considered a lake, it is the largest in Eurasia, but is divided between the two geographic continents)</li>
<li><b>Oceania</b> – <a href="/wiki/Lake_Eyre" title="Lake Eyre">Lake Eyre</a> when filled; the largest permanent (and freshwater) lake in Oceania is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Taupo" title="Lake Taupo">Lake Taupo</a>.</li>
<li><b>Europe</b> – <a href="/wiki/Lake_Ladoga" title="Lake Ladoga">Lake Ladoga</a>, followed by <a href="/wiki/Lake_Onega" title="Lake Onega">Lake Onega</a>, both in northwestern Russia.</li>
<li><b>North America</b> – <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan%E2%80%93Huron" title="Lake Michigan–Huron">Lake Michigan–Huron</a>, which is hydrologically a single lake. However, lakes <a href="/wiki/Lake_Huron" title="Lake Huron">Huron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan" title="Lake Michigan">Michigan</a> are usually considered separate lakes, in which case <a href="/wiki/Lake_Superior" title="Lake Superior">Lake Superior</a> would be the largest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manivanan2008a_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Manivanan2008a-59">[59]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>South America</b> – <a href="/wiki/Lake_Titicaca" title="Lake Titicaca">Lake Titicaca</a>, which is also the highest navigable body of water on Earth at 3,812 metres (12,507 ft) above sea level. The much larger <a href="/wiki/Lake_Maracaibo" title="Lake Maracaibo">Lake Maracaibo</a> is much older, but perceived by some to no longer be genuinely a lake for multiple reasons.</li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-Caspian-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Caspian_77-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> is generally regarded by geographers, biologists and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limnologist" title="Limnologist">limnologists</a> as a huge inland <a href="/wiki/Salt_lake" title="Salt lake">salt lake</a>. However, the Caspian's large size means that for some purposes it is better modeled as a sea. Geologically, the Caspian, <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> seas are remnants of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Ocean" title="Tethys Ocean">Tethys Ocean</a>. Politically, the distinction between a sea and a lake may affect how the Caspian is treated by international law.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
